o fa t m o s p h e r i cc h a r g e dp a r t i c l ed e n s i t y ,s i m u l a t i o n of t h e a t o m i c o x y g e n c o n c e n t r a t i o n c a n b e p e r f o r m e d u n d e r s t a t i c\no r dynamic c o n d i t i o n s .I nt h es t a t i c\ncase, s i m u l a t i o n i s r e s t r i c t e d t o\nt h e D r e g i o n and t h e e f f e c t i v e volume w i l l b e r e s t r i c t e d t o\na t i o n c a n b e s i m u l a t e d i n\na chamber\nwhose\na m b i e n tp r e s s u r ec o r r e s p o n d st ot h e\nE r e g i o n .T h i s\nis\naccomplishedby means o f a d i r e c t e d beam of oxygen which contains\n20\npercent\natomic\nand\n80 percentmolecularoxygen.Simulation\nof t h i s\np a r a m e t e rc a n n o tb er e a d i l y\ncombined w i t h c h a r g e d p a r t i c l e p r o d u c t i o n ,\nb u tp e r m i t ss i m u l t a n e o u ss i m u l a t i o n\nof t h e s o l a r X-rayanduvspectrum.\n\n76\n\nIV.\n\nDETECTION OF RADIATION\n\nNecessary prerequisites for the simulation of the D and E region are\nthe detection of radiation in the vacuum ultraviolet and X-ray regions and\nthe calibration of rhe detectors. Within the past fifteen years, many new\nvacuum ultraviolet light sources have been developed. Concurrently, these\nadvances have stimulated the development of new photoelectric detection\nmethods. Monochromatic detectors have not been developed; however, in some\nspectral regions, monochromatic detection can be approximated. Such narrow\nband detectors when combined with selective thin-film filters, window materials, and judicious use of\ngrating monochromators have advanced the state-ofthe-art. Progress has also been made in the field of absolute intensity\nmeasurements in the vacuum ultraviolet. Generally, the X-ray spectrum has\nbeen extensively studied and many pertinent properties are known. However,\nexperimental methods for the measurement of the absolute intensity of the\nsoft X-ray flux perLinent to simulation techniques are practically nonexistent.\nIn the following sections, the various types of detectors developed for\' extreme\nultraviolet and, where appropriate, soft X-ray regions, together with the\nlatest techniques for their\ncalibration, will be discussed.\nUltraviolet Spectrum\nThe detection of radiation below 20008 is discussed,in terms of: (1)\nphotoelectric effect, (2) photoionization of gases, and (3) fluorescence.\nBlackening of photographic plates is also an appropriate consideration;\nhowever ?\'.t will not be discussed further since it is the least versatile\nof the four.\nPhotoelectric Effect.- The "surface" photoelectric effect is small compared to the volume effect. The volume effect was unknown until the studies\nof Kenty,[65] in 1933, on several metals. Very little quantitative data\nhad been obtained until the work of Hinteregger, Watanabe and Weissler [ 6 6 , 6 7 ]\nwho twenty years later measured the yield of a metal down to 5008. These\nauthors obtained yields (electrons released per incident photon) of the order\nof 10 t o 15 percent. The lack of suitable light sources.and means for determining the absolute number of incident photons preventedfurther work to\nshorter wavelengths. Brunet, et al. [ 681 have made measurements below 5008,\nbut their results are relative. Using rare-gas ion-chambersy present studies\nat the GCA Corporation are measuring yields down to .2008 on anabsolute basis.\n..\n\n.\n\n.\n\n-\n\n.\n\nThe selectivity of the photoelectriceffect is effective in discriminating radiation above and below 1200g. Figure 25 shows a typical curve for\nthe absolute photoelectric efficiency .as a function of .wavelength\n,\n\n77\n\nU\n(x,\n\n: O/O\n0\n\nI8\n\nI6\n\no\\"\n- . 14\n0\n\nJ\nW\n\n-\n\n2 12\n.\n\n0\nU\nI-\n\n8\n\nIO\n\nJ\nW\n\n\' 8\n\n8\na\n\n6\n\n4\n\n2\n\n0\n\nI\n\n1\n300\n\n40\n0\n\n500\n\n600\n\n700\n\n800\n\n900\n\n1000\n\nWAVELENGTH 6 )"-t\nF i g u r e 25.\n\nAbsolute p h o t o e l e c t r i c f f i c i e n c y .\ne\n\nI1 0 0\n\nI200\n\nIXX)\n\n1\n\nof an aluminum cathode [69]. This curve i s typical of many metals indicating\nthat these cathodes are particularly suitable for use between 200 and 12008\nand can discriminate against both the X-rays and longer ultraviolet and\nvisible light. The use of more exotic cathodes such as thin laycrs of lithium\nfluoride and strontium fluoride on the other hand is expected to give higher\nyields in the 100 to 2002 region.\nPhotoionization of Gases. Experiments on photoionization of gases have\nbeen conducted since the turn of the century. In an original experiment,\nLenard [70] inferred that ultraviolet light from air ionized by spark was\na\ncapable of discharging an electroscope. However, subsequent measurements of\nion mobilitites indicated that the ions, particularly those produced unfilin\ntered air, were relatively large particles or\nnuclei and not molecular ions.\nIn order to eliminate large particles, Hughes [71] and others used carefully\nfiltered air; nevertheless, they found that ultraviolet radiation transmitted by fluorite (transmission limit about 1240g) was capable of producing\nions of molecular size butradiation transmitted by quart\'z was not effective.\nSince the ionization potentials of the consticuents of air correspond to\nwavelengths much lower than12408, the observed ions could not\nhave been\nproduced by direct photoionization, but possibly by a two step process,, or\nby ionization of some impurities such as mercury vapor, or by accelerated\nphotoelectrons. It is difficult to interpret the results o f these early\nexperiments, since undispersed radiation was used and quantum yields were not\nmeasured. Hughes and DuBridge [72] describe these experiments and explain\n"that the state of our knowledge regarding the ionization of these gases is\nextremely unsatisfactory".\nSeveral early investigators studied\nthe photoionization of the alkali\nvapors, taking advantage of the fact that these vapors\ncan be ionized in the\nregion 2000 to 3200g where quartz optics could be used. Experimental techniques, in particular, energy measurements, improved in studies utilizing\nmonochromatic radiation. Thus, many quantitative data were obtained, particularly by Mohler and co-workers [73] and by Lawrence and Edlefsen, [74].\nThe study of photoionization cross section of alkali vapors has been reviewed\nand summarized by several writers [72,75,76].\nStudies on the photoionization of gases almost ceased after 1930. I\nt\nwas not until 1950 that the first thermocouple\nmeasurement of dispersed\nvacuum ultraviolet radiation by Packer andLock [77] and its application\nto the absolutequantum yield of CaSO M phosphor by Watanabe [78] confirmed\n:n\nthe feasibility of photoionization an8 photoelectric studies in the spectral\nregion below 2000g with dispersed radiation obtained from a vacuum monochromator developed by Tousey, et al. [79]. In 1953, Watanabe, Marmo and Inn,\n[ 8 0 ] and Wainfan, Walker and Weissler [81] reported on photoionization measurements in the vacuum ultraviolet region. The former.group showed that accurate\ndetermination of the ionization potential is possibleby utilizing monochromatic light of 0.01 ev bandwidth, and ionization potentials for about 80\nmolecules have been reported [82,82]. Later, Sakai, Little, and Watanabe\n[82] reduced the bandwidth to 0.001 ev.\n\n79\n\nMore recently,GCA Corporation hascomp1e:ed the measurement of the\nyieids of some20 molecules in a study to find suitable gases for narrow\nband ionization chambers. To produce narrow spectral band sensitivities\nit\nis necessary to select the appropriate choiceof window materialand gas filling. The short wavelengthcut-off is determined by the window material while\n: e long wavelength cut-off is determined by the ionization potential of\nh\nthe\nSas. The technique for extending the sensitivity range of\nstandard photomultipliers down to the soft\nX-ray region is to coat the\nouter glass envelope with a material which fluoresce when illuminated by\nwill\nuv radiation. Compton. and Allison [lo31\nsuggest that absolute intensities are perhaps most easily found\nby measuring the heat produced from\nX-rays with, for example, a\nbolometer. Tomboulian\n[lo41 indicated that a specially constructed Geiger counter under a given\nsituation can measure absolute photon fluxes soft radiation. Preliminary\nof\ntheoretical calculations of absolute intensities,\nmade by Tomboulian [lo41\nmay be applied for calibration purposes. Generally, if any reliable\nfew\nmethods are available for the calibration of counters the soft X-ray region.\nin\nby\nThe 44 to 608 Geiger counter flown GCA Corporation [loo] was calibrated\nwith an irm-55 isotope source. This isotope emits a monochromatic X-ray at\n1.98 which lies within the higher energy\nwindow of the mylar counter seen\nas\nis\nin Figure 29. Since the source strength 5 millicuries it is possible to\nobtain relative efficiency measurements various aspect angles. Unfortunately,\nat\nan absolute efficiency calibration the counter for\nof\nthis wavelength i s not\npossible since the\nflux from the source is not\nknown accurately.\n\n104\n\nV.\n\nIONOSPHERIC SIMLTLATION FACILITY\n\nAs shown in the foregoing discussion, the simultaneous simulation of\nmany ionospheric parameters is possible. These include and X-ray radiation\nuv\nand the production charged particlesby either direct ionization the\nof\nof\ngas within the chamber or by injecting a space-charge neutralized particle\nbeam from a plasma gun exterior to the chamber. Similarly, it is possible\nto produce atomic oxygen either direct photodissociation of oxygen within\nby\nthe chamber or by producing atomic oxygen exterior the chamber and injectto\ning a beam containing a known mixture of atomic and molecular oxygen through\na port in the chamber wall.\nBoth the external plasma gun and the external atomic\noxygen generator\nare expensive and bulky, particularly because they require\'fairly complex\na\ndifferentially-pumped vacuum system. They are also expensive and need not be\nan integral partof the initial installation, provided adequate floor space\nis allowed and sufficient vacuum connecting ports the simulation chamber\non\nare provided for their eventual installation\nThe present discussion will, therefore, be confined to the design of the\nvacuum chamber proper, the vacuum system the equipment for the simulation\nand\nof charged particle and atomic oxygen densit\nies, and X-ray and uv radiation\nin.tegra1 with the ionospheric simulator.\nMuch thought has been\ngiven to the simulation chamber concept\nand in view\nof the complexity and cost of much of the associated equipment, it was felt\nthat considerations pertainingto ruggedness and easeof assembly and operation should govern the design.\nFor this reason, a metal vacuum chamber proposed with adequate conis\nnecting and viewing ports to permit accessibility and visual observation.\nHigh-quality stainless-steel vacuum components of great variety\nare now\navailable commercially\nand this typeof construction has considerable merit\ncompared with a glass chamber. In particular, it is a simple matterto change\nthe configuration of an experimental arrangement,all components are fitted\nif\nwith metal flanges which\nare readily interchangeable. With a glass chamber,\non the other hand, even small modifications require expert skillnotwithand\nstanding great care and patience, costly and time-consuming accidents\nwill\noccur.\nA stainless-steel chamber is not incompatible withof the proposed\nany\nmethods of simulation except\nwith the production of atomic oxygen by photodissociation. This latter experiment requires a liner of low recombination\nrate, such as pyrex or teflon which readily be inserted into\ncan\nthe chamber.\n\n105\n\nIt will be shown subsequentlythat a circular 1-meter diameter chamber\nwill permit the installation all instrumentation considered necessary.\nof\nFor adequate flexibility sixteen\n6-inch flanged ports are recommended which\nwill be staggered in two rows on the circumference. In addition, glass viewing ports which are commercially available many sizes can be mounted along\nin\nthe circumference or on top of the chamber. The concept of the complete simulation facility is\nshown in Figure 35.\n?he Vacuum System\nMethods of Pumping. The operating pressure of the simulation chamber\ncovers the region from approximately1 torr to 10-6 torr.\nWhereas the attainment of these pressures presents little difficulty\nwith conventional vacuum\npumps, it is considered desirable to provide a\npumping system which accident-proof and does not introduce undesirable\nis\nand uncontrolled contaminants.\nDuring the past decade it been established conclusively that both\nhas\nmechanical eccentric rotor pumps and oil diffusion pumps, no how well\nmatter\na\ntrapped, can seriously contaminatethe various components of vacuum system\nand affect the mechanical and electrical performance of test objects placed\nin such systems. Furthermore, accidental exposure hot pump oil to atmosof\na\npheric pressure may necessitate lengthy and laborious cleaning procedure\ncostly in time and manpower.\nAlthough liquid-nitrogen-trapped mercury diffusion pumps can operate\nin\ncompletely contamination free fashion,\nthey require a constant supply of liquid\nnitrogen (the vapor pressure of mercury is approximately tor\nat room\ntemperature).\nFurthermore, accidents are likely to be far more serious in\nview of the toxic\nnature of mercury vapor.\n\nIt is fortunate that the state of the vacuum art progressed to a\nhas\npoint where mostof these difficultiescan be overcome in simple manner.\na\nReduction from atmospheric pressure below 1 torr in completely oil-free\nfashion can be attained bymeans of liquid-nitrogen chilled zeolite sorption\npump. For example, a sorption pump containing 2 lb of zeolite after pre0\nchilling, can evacuate a 100-liter volumeto 0.1 torr in less than 1 minutes.\nThe limitation of zeolite sorption pumps is their inability pump helium\nto\nand\nand neon, whose partial pressures in the atmosphere are 1.4 x\n4X\ntorr, respectively. Thus, simple sorption pumping cannot reduce\nthe\ntotal\npressure torr.\nbelow\nOil-free pumping in the range from 10 torr to torr\ncan however, be\nperformed by mechanical pumps which employ\nthe Roots principle. They are\nrelatively simple in construction, consisting primarily two "figure-eight\'\'\nof\nintermeshing rotors revolving in a close-fitting housing,and a motor. The\nrotors counter-rotate and are synchronized by a pair of timing gears mounted\non the rotor shafts.\n106\n\nM\n\nPORT\nIDENTIFICATION\nTABLE\n\nDIFFERENTIAL PUMPING\nSECTION\n\nx\n\nGRATING\n\n3\n\nMONOCHROMATOR\n\n4\n\n5\nATOMIC\n\nBEAM\nBEAM\n\n6\n7\n\nPORT\n\nATOMIC\n\nPORT\n\n8\n\nx\n\nCHARGED PARTICLES\nPORT\n-CHARGED\nPARTICLES\n\nI\n\nPORT\n12\n\nAUXILIARY\nPORT\n\nPOWER\nSUPPLY\n\nI\n\n[\n\nRECORDER\n\nI\n\nDIFFERENTIAL\nPUMPING\nSECTION\n\n1\n4\n\n1\n5\n\nCALIBRATION\nCHARGED PAR\nTICLES PORT\n\n1\n6\n\nCALIBRATION\nATOMIC BEA\nPORT\n\nMONOCHROMATO$(/\n\nDIFFERENTIAL/+^\n\nGRATING\nPUMPING\nSECTION\n\n1\n3\n\nSUPPLY\n\nI\n\n1025.7 EXPERMENT\nCALIBRATION, ATOMIC BEAM\nEXPERIMENT\nCALIBRATION. CHARGED\nPARTICLES\nEXPERIMENT\nCALIBRATION,CHARGED\nPARTICI FS FXPFRIMENT\nxz937.8\nEXPERIMENT\nX-RAY EXPERIMENT\n1215.7\nEXPERIMENT\n\n8\n\nx:\n\na\nSUPPLY\nPOWER\n\nRECORDER\n\nFigure 35.\n\nIonospheric simulator block diagram\n\n8\n\n977.0\nEXPERIMENT\nATOMIC BEAM EXPERIMENT\nATOMIC BEAM EXPERIMENT\nCHARGED PARTICLES EXPERIMENT\nCHARGED PARTICLES EXPERIMENT\nAUXILIARY PORT\nDETECTOR X-RAY EXPERIMENT\nSTD ION CHAMBER DETECTOR\nFOR I I\n\nI\n2\n\n1\n\ni\n\nAlthough Roots pumps\ncan discharge directly to atmosphere without backing\npumps to produce a rough vacuum, this procedure is not recommended. The\naddition ofa backing pump makes possible to operate the Roots pump at\nit\nlower inlet pressures; the low pressure limit under these conditions is about\n10-5 torr. The rotors of Roots pumps are precision machined to insure\na\nminute clearance between\nthe rotors themselves and between the rotors and the\nchamber wall. As a result, no sealing or lubricating oil is needed in the\npumping chamber. In consequence, backmigration of pumping fluid is essentially\neliminated. For ultimate cleanliness,a liquid-nitrogen cooledtrap may be\nprovided at the inlet of the Roots pump and dry-ice or zeolite trap ahead\na\nof the backing pump.\n\nWhereas Roots pumps operate efficiently over the pressure region covering\nD\nthe whole of the Region and the lowerE region, they are not capable of\nefficiently reproducing the pressure of the upper Region. This limitation\nE\nof\nmay be acceptable for initial operation the ionospheric simulator. However, if evacuation to lower .pressures is\ndesired, this can also be accomplished in accident-proof and fluid-free fashion. Titanium sputter-ion pumps\nfunction very efficiently in this pressure region. Some of the ions\nand\nexcited atoms producedin the electrical discharge of these pumps\ncan, however,\n.migrateinto the simulation chamber. No such problems arise with turbomolecular mechanical pumps which perform satisfactorily from about to\n10-3\nbelow 10-9 torr. These pumps consist ofa st.ator and rotor made up of discs\nof\ninto which are milled inclined radial slits. The rotational speed the\nrotor is 16,000 rpm. For optimum performancea forevacuum of less than\n5 x\ntorr is required, which, as in the case of Roots pumps, is provided\nby a trapped eccentric rotor pump.\nA pumping system consisting of single eccentric rotor pump which\na\nalternately backs a Roots pump or a turbo-molecular pump via suitable valving\nappears to be the most\nattractive solutionto the problem outlined above.\nFor initial evacuation from atmospheric pressure into the range to\ntorr,\none or more commercial liquid-nitrogen-chilled sorption pumps can be to\nadded\nthe system.\nVacuum Gauges. The pressure range to be monitored includes the difficult\nregion between 1 torr and torr\nwhere, until recently no reliable commer10-3 torr, a wide\ncial continuously-reading monitors were available. Below\nvariety of good commercial ionization gauges be obtained.\ncan\na\nThe McLeod gauge, spite of its excellent accuracy, is not very conin\nvenient pressure monitor, and any case, doesnot lend itself to automatic\nin\npressure control. Fortunately, high accuracy of measurement and pressure\ncontrol can be obtained with a commerical capacitance manometer. Unlike other\ntypes of pressuremonitors (ionization or thermal conductivity gauges), a\ncapacitance manometer gives pressure indication independent of the species\na\n2\nof gas to an accuracy better than percent full scale over the pressure range\nfrom 1000 torr to 5 x\ntorr. Below this level, an ionization gauge is the\nof\nmost reliable pressure monitor. The output signal the capacitance manometer or the ionization gauge be used toactuate a commercial pressure\ncan\n\n108\n\ncontroller which can maintain the pressure, via a variable gas leak, at a\n0.\nconstant value with less than3 percent full scale random drift per day.\nSimulation of\n\nCharged\n\nParticles\n\nA s discussed in some detail Section 111, the production of charged\nin\nparticles under conditions thermal equilibriumcan be realistically simuof\nlated over only a limited-altitude range. With available sources of ultraviolet radiation,as discussed in the following section, the charged particle\ndensity can be produced directly by photoionization a gas pressure of\nat\n100 p , corresponding to an altitude of 80 km.\n\nAt other altitudes, the production of charged particles photoionization\nby\ncannot be performed under conditions which simulate other ionospheric parameters\nrealistically.\nCompromise solutions can include fast electron gun.or a plasma gun as\na\ndiscussed in Section 111, which will require differential pumping. Sincethe\ndesign of these devices has been adequately documented in the literature\nreferred to previously, they can be added to thesimulation facilityat some\nlater date. For this purpose, an adequate number of6-inch flanged ports have\nbeen made available on the proposed simulation chamber.\nAnother area, which is also widely documented is that of charged particle\ndetectors, which range from simple ionization chambers measurement of\nfor\ntotal charge producedto chargedparticle spectrometers of varying degrees\nof\nsophistication. A decision on the most suitable type\nof instrumentation will\ndepend on the types of experiments proposed and needbe made in the initial\nnot\nplanning phase. The chamber dimensions have been chosen\n\'sufficiently large\nt o accommodate all types of charged particle detectors in current use.\nSimulation of Important Vacuum Ultraviolet Solar Lines\nThe conceptual design the experimental facilits shown in Figure\nof\n35\nincludes the latest techniques simulate the important vacuum and extreme\nto\nultraviolet solar lines and is the result of detailed analysis\nof the relative\nmerits of pertinent experimental apparatus. Not readily evident is theabsence of the He I1 304 2 light source. It was decided to omit this light\nsource altogether, because produces the least important the series of solar\nit\nof\nregion (depth\nultraviolet emission lines necessary for the simulation of E the\nat unit optical thickness is\n182 km,\nwhich lies in the F1 region) and because\nthe experimental difficulties of simulating this line are severe. It requires\na spark discharge source which not necessary for simulating the other solar\nis\nlines. The photon fluxes emitted are not very intense and are further reduced\nif a monochromator is used, since the reflectivities the various gratings\nof\nthat are available today are poor below 2. In the following sections,\n400\nthe\nsalient features of\nthe instrumentation are discussed in some detail.\n\n109\n\nVacuum Ultraviolet Monochromators. In Section IV it was shown that some\nlight sources could be\nmade nearly monochromaticby means of gas and/or thin\nmetal filters. This was suggested as a means to separate the Lyman f3 (1025.72)\nand the Lyman E (937.82) atomic lines in the hydrogen spectrum without having\nto use two monochromators. For the C I11 (977.08) line, on the other hand, a\nmonochromator was deemed essential since it is not possible to separate this\nline without allowing other equally intense impure lines\nto be emitted also.\nAfter considering the expected loss of intensityto the various\ndue\nloss of spectral purity, it became obvious that a\nfilters and the resulting\ndispersive device was the best approach to separate\nLyman f3 and Lyman E. Once\nthis was decided, detailed studies\nwere made concerning various designs\nof\nvacuum ultraviolet monochromators. One approach was based a separate\non\nchamber, containinga single grating along 0.5m or l-meter Rowland circle\na\nfrom which all pertinent wavelengths could be focussed into one port\nof the\nionospheric simulation chamber. Calculations indicated that, for this purpose, 5 light sources would have to be positioned on the circumference of the\nRowland circle separated bya distance of 4 degrees, that is, 10 cm. Although,\nat first glance, this seemed impracticable, further study indicated the\nthat\ncapillaries of the various light sources (whose diameter is less 1 cm)\nthan\nmight be stacked sufficiently closely to meet this restriction. However, a\nfirm design could not be worked out without further laboratory studies.\nAnother approach considered was the stacking of two gratings in a\n5-mm\nsingle monochromator for the separation of 937.82 and 1025.72 atomic\nthe\nhydrogen lines. However, the cost of designing and constructingthis special\nmonochromator will be greater than the cost of the more conventional instrumentation proposed below.\nThe final design consists three monochromators which approximately\nof\nduplicate the arrangement by Seya and Namioka [105]. It was chosen mainly\nbecause the 35-degree angle of incidence results in favorable intensities\nwithin the prescribed geometry. This configuration is not quite as compact\nas a monochromator where the angle of incidence is increased degrees\nto 70\nand where the entrance and exit slits be nearly in line close the axis.\ncan\nto\nCalculations showed thatin the latter case, the beam wouldbe attenuated by\nto\na factor of 3; furthermore, an instrument of this type would have be\nspecially designed and constructed.\nm e\nfor the monochromators\nthree\nare as follows:\nthe\ndiameter of the Rowland circle 0.5 m with a grating (platinized) of 1200 lines/mm\nis\nand blazed at 7502. Simple rotation of the grating will give the desired line\nyet maintain good focusing of the image. A resolution of 12 is then available\nwhich is more than adequate for line emission light sources. fast 4-inch\nA\noil diffusion pump (backed by 13 CFM mechanical pump) which produces a\na\ncan be maintained under\nvacuum of 10-5 torr should be provided. This pressure\noperating conditions, i.e., with windowless light sources, since\na differential\none of the monopumping section is available. It is suggested that at least\nchromators be equipped with drive mechanismso that the entire wavelength\na\nregion of interest\ncan be scanned by rotating the grating.\n110\n\nUltraviolet Detectors and Calibration Techniques.\nThe proposed detectors\noutlined below represent the present state of the art has progressed\nwhich\nconsiderably in recent years. They include ionization chambers and photoelectric detectors. The various calibration techniques\nare outlined in some\ndetail.\nIonization chambers. The ionization chamber is commonly used when\nan\nabsolute radiant flux measurement is required. conventional chamber has\nThe\ntwo parallel plates,\none the ion collector and the other the ion repeller.\nThe ions are formed a region where the electric field is uniform\nin\nand\nsufficiently large to collect all ions but not large enough to cause ion\nproduction by secondary electron collisions. Using,the measured ion current\nand the known photoionization yieldof the filling gas,. the absolute radiant\nflux can be determined.\nA simpler and more compact ion chamber be constructed out a metal\ncan\nof\ncylinder and central collector pin. This construction is commonly called a\nphoton counter, [106]. Such counters are suitable as narrowband detectors\nof\nabsolute radiant flux in the vacuum ultr,aviolet region of the spectrum. Their\nbandwidth lies between the wavelength corresponding the ionization potential\nto\nof the gas and the shortwave limit of transmission f the window. For example,\no\na counter filled with nitric oxide gas with a lithium fluoride window is sensitive from 13502 (ionization potentialof NO) to 10508 (the limit of transmission of lithiumfluoride).\nAs a detector of absolute radiant\nflux, the photon countercan be applied\nas a secondary standard in the laboratoryasor narrowband detector as\na\napplied by NASA and the . S . Naval Research Laboratoryto determine the\nU\nabsolute radiant flux from sun in the vicinity of the Lyman-a line\nthe\n(1215.72). For the ionospheric simulator, this photon\nco\'unter is proposed\nfor the detection of Lyman-a (1215.72 using nitric\noxide as the filling gas.\nExperiments have shown that the effect of impurities in nitric oxide\non\nthe photoionization current for\nLyman-a is only one percent. Since the\nphoton counter is used for detecting only\nLyman-a (1215.72), the effect of\nimpurities is not detrimental; in\nfact, it may even be beneficial since the\nphotoionization currentat wavelengths otherthay Lyman-a is decreased.\nHowever, the unpurified gas is probably more corrosive than pure nitric oxide\nand this shouldbe borne in mind.\nCalibration of NO-filled photon counters\nand 07 filled ionization\nchambers. It is known that calibrated NO-filled photon counters can be purchased on the open market, however, since all the instrumentation.necessary\nto calibrate these counters will be available as of other apparatus, it\npart\nis strongly suggested that this calibration be performed situ.\nin\n\nTo calibrate the photon counters,\nthe total photon flux emerging from\nthe exit slitof the monochromator is measured with an ionization chamber\nfilled with nitric\noxide gas. From the known photoionization yield [ 8 0 ] of\nNO the radiant flux is determined.This flux is then compared with the\n111\n\nor\n\nSubsequent counterscan be calibrated directly against the one initially\ncalibrated against the ion chamber.\nThe detection and calibration of Lyman 1 0 2 5 . 7 2 ) can be performed by one\nB (\nof two methods. The first utilizes the photoionization yield of\noxygen, the\nsecond depends on the photoelectric yield ofa metal. The principles of and\nprocedures for the calibration an ionization chamber filled with oxygen\nof\nas\nfor LymanB are essentially the same that described forLyman a except that\nfor Lyman p, the conditions arewindowless, and thus differential pumping is\nnecessary. The dimensions of the ionization chambercan be the same as those\nmentioned above or can be made smaller if necessary. For all other wavelengths, namely,\nand 3 0 3 . 8 x Y the metallic photoelectric detector serves\nnot only as a detector o f radiation but also as a secondary standard. The\nprocedure, which is as follows, is quite simple.sample of aluminum or\nA\nplatinum (untreated), large enough to intercept the light beam near the\nor benzene before placing\nentrance of the light source is cleaned with acetone\nit in the system. It is mounted in front of the light source, by means of a\ntraverse rod which allows the metal to be moved\nin and out of the light beam\nat will without breaking the vacuum. circular wire loop is mounted directly\nA\nin front of the metal surface to collect the photoelectrons ejected. With\nthe light sourceon, the photoelectric current the metal sample is measured\nof\nby increasing the voltage between the loop the surface untilall electrons\nand\nare collected. The absolute photonflux, P, can then be calculated by the\nformula:\n1 0 2 5 . 7 ,9 3 7 . 8 ,5 8 4 . 5\n\ny = -i/e\n\nP\n\n(34)\n\nwhere Y is the photoelectric yield of the metal a at\ngiven wavelength,i is\nthe current in amperes and is the electronic charge. The specific experimental details of\nthis\nmethod are given in the literature [ 8 5 ] .\nVacuum Ultraviolet Light Sources.For the simulation ofthe various\nsolar ultraviolet emission lines, at least three different light source\ndesigns are proposed, result of detailed studies optimize performance\na\nto\nand to facilitate adequate instrumentation.\n\n114\n\nMicrowave discharge lamp. For Lyman a , (1215.72) , the microwave discharge lamp is the most applicable. The microwave light source simply a\nis\nlong quartz tube placed within a microwave cavity.\nThe quartz tube in\nthe\ncavity has to be forced-air-cooledto dissipate the intense heat generated\nby the discharge. The quartz tube is sealed a Teflon ferrule via a\nby\n"Swagelok" fitting to a water-cooled flange. A 2450 Mc, 125-watt microwave\ngenerator is used to excite the cavity. By adjusting the tuning stub, maximum\ncoupling light intensity can be achieved. The hydrogen spectrum produced in\nthe microwave cavity is similar that of the dc glow and hot-filament arcto\ndischarges, discussed below, although somewhat weaker.\nLike the hot filament\narc, the microwave discharge in hydrogen tends favor the atomic spectra.\nto\nIn a hydrogen-heliummixture, the microwave discharge produces perhaps\nthe\nmost monochromatic source of Lyman-a radiation. The advantages of an electrodeless discharge are the absence of sputtered electrode material and impurities\nembedded within the electrodes. This, in turn, prolongs the life of\nthe\nwindows.\nThe radiation is transmitted through a window of lithium fluoride. The\nplates are about 2 mm thick and 25 mm in diameter. Cleaved plates are preferred to polished plates because of their superior transmission\ncharacteristics. The action of the discharge and/or the formation of F-centers on prolonged use causes accumulative\nloss of transmittance, requiring periodic\nreplacement of the window. Rapid and convenient replacement can be achieved\nby the O-ring design shown Figure 37 dueto Warneck [107]; the window is\nin\nplaced between two butyl-rubber\nO-rings, located in grooved brass flanges.\nHot filament discharge lamp. For the simulation ofthe 1025.7, 977.0,\nand 937.8x lines, the hot filament arc discharge lamp been found to be\nhas\nmore efficient than other available light sources.\nFor the two higher members\nof the Lyman series of atomichydrogen, namely, @ and E , a mixture of hydrogen\nand helium produces the maximum intensity. For the C 111, 977.08 line, carbon dioxide with a trace oxygen produces the best results. A general\nof\ndescription and the advantages of this lamp follows.\nThe use of hot filaments to provide the free electrons necessary\nto\nsustain a discharge in hydrogen and other gases has been described\nin the\nliterature [108,109]. A hot-filament arc discharge can support discharge\na\ncurrent of several amperes at low voltage, typically 50 to 100 V. Thus,\nthere is no need for a high voltage power\nsupply. A typical 150 V\n5 A\npower supply is shown in\nFigure 38 which can easily constructed by most\nbe\nlaboratories. Should a higher degreeof current stabilizationbe required,\nan excellent circuit is given by\nMoak, et al. [110]. Although periodic\nrenewal of the filament is required, this does not present any particular\ndifficulties.\n\n-\n\nThe proposed hot filament arc source shown\nin Figure 39 is due to\nHartman [109,111]. The filament is a helically coiled nickel\nribbon dipped\nin barium carbonate and activated in a hydrogen atmosphere.\nA trigger pin\nis inserted in the quartz discharge tube strike the arc initially.A\nto\nTesla coil issuitable,to trigger the discharge. The intensity ratio is only a factor of\ntwo or three; however, the ratio of atomic to molecular line intensities is\nmuch greater for the hot filament arc discharge.\nHelium plasma light source.\nA simple but intense helium 30-watt light\nsource has recently been developed which can 6mit more than\nphotons/sec\n(1015 photons/sec/cm )2 at 584g. The details of this source as developed b y\nJensen and Libby [I121 are as follows:\nA thoriated tungsten wire, the hot cathode and a tantalum wire, the anode\nare encaged in a large metal cannister. Typical operating conditions are a\nI1elium pressure of 300 microns and an applied potential of 30 V, with 1 A\nflowing. The exact source dimensions and geometry are not critical, however,\nthe following arrangement has been suggested: a cathode of 0.010-inch\ndiameter, thoriated tungsten wire, 3 cm long, parallel to and 1 cm from a\n0.020-inch diameter tantalum wire, 3 cm long, serving as anode. The device\nhas several current modes depending on cathode temperature, anode voltage\nand helium pressure. The low-current mode is equivalent to ordinary operation\nof a vacuum diode tube; the intermediate-current mode of hundreds of milliamperes to a few tens of amperes is the condition in which the device operates\nas presently described; the high-current mode is a typical low-pressure arc.\nIf the pressure drops to less than 50 microns, if the cathode temperature is\ntoo low to cause significant thermionic emission, or if the anode voltage\nfalls to less than 22 V, the device operates into the low-current mode. The\ntransition is gradual in ttle case of varying cathode temperaturebut very\nabrupt when pressure or anode voltage are varied. A drop of less than 0.1 V,\nfor example, will cause the current to fall from manyamperes to a few milliamperes. With very pure helium and well-out-gassed electrodes the process is\nreversible; otherwise there is a hysterisis effect and a higher starting\nvoltage is necessary, although again the current rises from milliamperes to\namperes over a 0.1-V potential change. In the intermediate-current mode,\nanode current is determined primarily by cathode temperature. Anode voltage\nhas little effect once it has been raised past the strikinglevel. Separation\nbetween cathode and anode can be increased to 7 cm with no appreciable increase\nin required anode voltage. The factor which limits the current is the amount\nof heat which the anode and the lead-in conductors can dissipate. In the\nintermediate-current mode the source resistance is positive and thereforeno\nballast resistance is required for stable operation. With a very stable\nlow-output-impedance power supply there is no evidence of oscillationsup to\nfrequencies of 100 Mc/sec. Apart from a simple gas flow inlet-system, no\nequipment additional to a xenon lamp producing radiation at\n14712 is required.\nHowever, as stated previously, liner of low recombination rate, such as\na\npyrex or teflon will have be inserted into the simulation chamber for this\nto\nexperiment.\nThe measurement of the density atomic oxygencan be performed by the\nof\nwell-known titration method\nfirst suggested bySpealman and Rodebush [115]\nand successfully used Kaufman [116] and also by\nby\nHerron and Schiff 11171.\nHerzog [118] has recently undertaken an investigation into the absolute\nmeasurement of atomic oxygen concentration observing the heat of recomby\nbination in a recombination cell.
